The Ash'ari Argument on Power and Its Refutation
Razi refutes the Ash'ari view on power by questioning its temporal relationship to the act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari argument for distinguishing voluntary from involuntary action and the logical objections raised against it regarding the timing of power.

Core Idea
The distinction of voluntary action hinges on power, but its timing is contested.

This lesson introduces the problem of power (qudra) in human actions. The Ash'aris claim that voluntary movement is distinguished by an attribute called power, but opponents question when exactly that distinction exists. The passage presents two sets of dilemmas that challenge the Ash'ari view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the argument structure. Note the initial definition of power and the Ash'ari claim.
2
Identify the two objections
Notice that two different objections are raised: one about the timing relative to the act, and another about the timing relative to God's creation.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Why does Razi conclude that choice is not established? Consider the impossibility of power existing before or during the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها القدرة والمرجع بها في حقنا إن كان إلى سلامة الأعضاء فهو معقول وإن كان إلى أمر وراثها فقيه النزاع . احتج أصحابنا بأن حركة المختار متميزة عن حركة المرتعش وليس الامتياز إلا بهذه الصفة . فيقال لهم : متى ثبت هذا الامتياز قبل الاتصاف بالفعل أو حال الانصاف . والأول باطل على قولك لأنك لا نثبت القدرة قبل الفعل ، والثانى كذلك لأن المرتعش كما لا يتمكن من ترك الحركة حال وجودها فالمختار لا يتمكن أيضا من تركها حال وجودها لاستحالة أن يكون الشىء معدوما موجودا في زمان واحد ، ويقال أيضا متى ثبت هذا الامتياز حال ما خلق الله تعالى الحركة أو قبلها، والأول باطل لأن حصول الفعل حال ماخلق الله تعالى ضرورى ، والثانى باطل لأن حصولها قبل أن خلقها الله تعالى محال وعلى التقديرين لا يثبت الاختبار (1) ويقال للمعتزلة متى ثبت هذا الاختيار عند استواء الدواعي أو عند رجحان أحدهما على الآخر ، الأول باطل، لأن عند الاستواء يمتنع الفعل وعند الامتناع لا تثبت للمكنة ، والثانى محال ، لأن عند حصول المرجح يجب الراجح ويمتنع المرجوح ، وعلى هذا التقدير لا نثبت المسكنة (٢)
(1) أقول : قوله و المختار لا يتمكن من الحركة حال وجود الحركة ، فيه نظر لأن المختار لا يتمكن من الحركة مع فرض وجود الحركة أما مع قطع النظر عنه فلم لا يجوز ، وهكذا القول في الاعتراض الثاني فإن الاختيار حال ماخلق الله الحركة محال بفرض وجود الحركة أما مع قطع النظر عن ذلك فممكن لوجود القدرة المقتضية له
الجائع
M
ولعله
(۲) أقول : الاختيار عند المعتزلة موضحة صدور الفعل أو تركه من القادر تبعا لداعية أو عدم داعية وهو متساوى النسبة إلى الطرفين عند عدم اعتبار الداعى وغير متساويهما عند اعتبار أحدهما . ومتقدموهم جوزوا صدور أحد الطرفين من المختار من غير ترجيح أحدهما على الآخر ، وأوردوا أمثلة والعطشان والهارب إذا حضر هم رغيفان متساويان وقد حان متساويان وطريقان متساويان فإنهم يختارون أحدهما من غير ترجيح ، والذين لا يجوزون ذلك يقولون الرجحان شيء والعلم بالرجحان شيء ، يختار أحدهما لوجود الرجحان وإن لم يفطن بالرجحان . ومتأخروهم قالوا بوجوب الرجحان وقال بعضهم بأن الطرف الراجح يكون أولى ولا ينتهى إلى حد الوجوب وهو اختيار « محمود الملاى ، وأنكر بعضهم كون الأولوية كافية لمثل مامر في خواص الممكن ، وأبو الحسين وأصحابه قالوا عند الداعى يجب الفعل وعند عدمه ممتنع وذلك لا ينافي الاختيار ، فإن تفسير الاختيار هو أن يكون الفعل والترك بالقياس إلى القدرة متساويين وبالقياس إلى الداعي وعدمه إما واجب أو ممتنع ، و من عدم التمييز بين الأمرين فى هذه المسألة يحدث الاختلاف الجارى بين القائلين بالإيجاب والاختيار. ( ١٤ - المحصل )
And among them is power (qudra), and its reference in our context: if it is to soundness of limbs, it is intelligible; if it is to something beyond that, there is dispute in it. Our companions (Ash'aris) argued that the movement of the free agent (mukhtar) is distinguished from the movement of the trembling person (murtacish), and the distinction is only by this attribute. So it is said to them: When is this distinction established? Before being qualified with the act or at the time of qualification? The first is false according to your own statement, because you do not affirm power before the act. The second is likewise false, because just as the trembling person cannot stop the movement during its existence, so the free agent also cannot stop it during its existence, because it is impossible for a thing to be nonexistent and existent in one time. And it is also said: When is this distinction established? At the time God creates the movement or before it? The first is false because the occurrence of the act at the time God creates it is necessary; the second is false because its occurrence before God creates it is impossible. On both suppositions, choice is not established. (1) And it is said to the Mu'tazila: When is this choice established? When motives are equal or when one of them outweighs the other? The first is false, because when they are equal the act is impossible, and when impossible capability is not established. The second is false, because when the preponderant motive occurs, the preponderant becomes necessary and the outweighed impossible, and on this supposition we do not establish capability. (2)
(1) I say: His statement 'the free agent cannot move during the existence of movement' is questionable, because the free agent cannot move under the assumption of movement's existence, but disregarding that, why should it not be possible? Likewise for the second objection: choice at the time God creates movement is impossible under the assumption of movement's existence, but disregarding that, it is possible due to the existence of power that necessitates it. [الجائع M ولعله]
(2) I say: Choice (ikhtiyar) according to the Mu'tazila is the clarification of the performance or leaving of the act by the capable one following a motive or lack of motive, and it is equal in relation to the two sides when no motive is considered, and unequal when one of them is considered. And their early scholars allowed the performance of one of the two sides by the free agent without preponderance of one over the other, and they gave examples: the thirsty person and the fleeing person when two equal loaves are present to them, and two equal times, and two equal paths; they choose one without preponderance. Those who do not allow that say: preponderance is one thing and knowledge of preponderance is another; they choose one due to the existence of preponderance even if they are not aware of it. Their later scholars said that preponderance is necessary, and some said that the preponderant side is more fitting without reaching necessity—this is the choice of Mahmud al-Mulla'. And some denied that sufficiency of priority for such cases in the properties of the possible. Abu al-Husayn and his companions said: with the motive, the act is necessary, and without it impossible, and that does not negate choice. For the interpretation of choice is that the act and its leaving are equal in relation to power, and in relation to the motive and its absence are either necessary or impossible. And from the failure to distinguish between the two matters in this question arises the ongoing disagreement between those who affirm necessitation and those who affirm choice. (14 - al-Muhassal)
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a complex theological discussion about the nature of power (قدرة) and choice (اختيار) in human actions, involving both Ash'ari and Mu'tazili positions, followed by explanatory footnotes. The text begins by stating that the reference of power (قدرة) in human context: if it means soundness of limbs, it is intelligible; if it means something beyond that, there is dispute. The Ash'aris (أصحابنا) argue that the movement of a free agent (مختار) is distinct from the involuntary movement of a trembling person (مرتعش), and this distinction is due to an attribute (power). The opponent then poses two sets of questions. First: when is this distinction established? Before the act (i.e., before the agent is qualified with the act) or at the time of qualification? The first is false on the Ash'ari's own principle because they do not affirm power before the act; the second is false because during the act, the trembling person cannot stop the movement, and similarly the free agent cannot stop it (since it is impossible for the same movement to both exist and not exist at the same time). Second: is the distinction established at the moment God creates the movement or before it? Both alternatives are problematic; thus choice (اختيار) is not established. The text then turns to the Mu'tazila and asks: when is choice established? When motives (دواعي) are equal or when one outweighs? If equal, the act is impossible, so capability is not established; if one outweighs, the preponderant act becomes necessary, again negating capability. The footnotes provide clarifications. The first footnote notes that the objection about the free agent being unable to move during the movement is questionable because it assumes the act's existence; disregarding that, it is possible. The second footnote gives a detailed account of the Mu'tazili view of choice: choice is the ability to perform or leave the act following a motive; it is equal in relation to both sides when no motive is considered, but not equal when a motive is considered. Early Mu'tazila allowed the performance of one of two equal alternatives without preponderance, citing examples like a thirsty person choosing between two equal loaves. Later Mu'tazila required a preponderant motive, with some saying it is necessary, others that the preponderant is merely more fitting. Abu al-Husayn and his followers said that with a motive the act is necessary and without it impossible, but this does not negate choice because choice is defined as equality of power in relation to both act and omission, while the motive determines necessity. The note concludes that failure to distinguish between these two aspects (power and motive) leads to the disagreement between determinists and proponents of free will.

Key Terms
قدرة
مختار
مرتعش
اختبار

Checkpoint

A) Because the Ash'aris do not affirm power before the act.
B) Because the free agent cannot stop the movement.
C) Because God creates the movement.
D) Because the trembling person can stop.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari attempt to ground voluntary action in power faces logical difficulties regarding whether power can exist simultaneously with the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, how often do you assume you have free will without considering the source of your power to act? This lesson shows that if your power is simultaneous with the act, you cannot stop it. Commit to identifying one action today where you felt you 'couldn't help it' and examine whether that was truly free or constrained.
?
When you make a moral choice, where does your power reside? Before the act? During? This lesson challenges the very timing of power. Reflect on a specific sin you committed recently. At that moment, could you have stopped? If not, does that change how you view your accountability? Write down one step to avoid that sin tomorrow by addressing the timing of your will.

The Mu'tazili View of Choice and Its Refutation
Razi raises a dilemma against the Mu'tazili concept of choice based on the state of motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazili view of choice and the objection that either equal motives or a preponderant motive undermines the possibility of choice.

Core Idea
If motives are equal, action is impossible; if one outweighs, action is necessary — so choice is lost.

This lesson presents a classic objection to the Mu'tazili theory of choice (ikhtiyar). The Mu'tazila hold that choice involves the ability to act based on motives. The opponent asks: when exactly does choice occur? The answer leads to a dilemma that seems to negate free will.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the dilemma. Notice the two alternatives: equal motives or preponderant motive.
2
Analyze each horn
Understand why equal motives make action impossible, and why a preponderant motive makes action necessary.
3
Conclude
The opponent claims that choice is not established on either view. Reflect on why.

Source Text

ومنها القدرة والمرجع بها في حقنا إن كان إلى سلامة الأعضاء فهو معقول وإن كان إلى أمر وراثها فقيه النزاع . احتج أصحابنا بأن حركة المختار متميزة عن حركة المرتعش وليس الامتياز إلا بهذه الصفة . فيقال لهم : متى ثبت هذا الامتياز قبل الاتصاف بالفعل أو حال الانصاف . والأول باطل على قولك لأنك لا نثبت القدرة قبل الفعل ، والثانى كذلك لأن المرتعش كما لا يتمكن من ترك الحركة حال وجودها فالمختار لا يتمكن أيضا من تركها حال وجودها لاستحالة أن يكون الشىء معدوما موجودا في زمان واحد ، ويقال أيضا متى ثبت هذا الامتياز حال ما خلق الله تعالى الحركة أو قبلها، والأول باطل لأن حصول الفعل حال ماخلق الله تعالى ضرورى ، والثانى باطل لأن حصولها قبل أن خلقها الله تعالى محال وعلى التقديرين لا يثبت الاختبار (1) ويقال للمعتزلة متى ثبت هذا الاختيار عند استواء الدواعي أو عند رجحان أحدهما على الآخر ، الأول باطل، لأن عند الاستواء يمتنع الفعل وعند الامتناع لا تثبت للمكنة ، والثانى محال ، لأن عند حصول المرجح يجب الراجح ويمتنع المرجوح ، وعلى هذا التقدير لا نثبت المسكنة (٢)

(۲) أقول : الاختيار عند المعتزلة موضحة صدور الفعل أو تركه من القادر تبعا لداعية أو عدم داعية وهو متساوى النسبة إلى الطرفين عند عدم اعتبار الداعى وغير متساويهما عند اعتبار أحدهما . ومتقدموهم جوزوا صدور أحد الطرفين من المختار من غير ترجيح أحدهما على الآخر ، وأوردوا أمثلة والعطشان والهارب إذا حضر هم رغيفان متساويان وقد حان متساويان وطريقان متساويان فإنهم يختارون أحدهما من غير ترجيح ، والذين لا يجوزون ذلك يقولون الرجحان شيء والعلم بالرجحان شيء ، يختار أحدهما لوجود الرجحان وإن لم يفطن بالرجحان . ومتأخروهم قالوا بوجوب الرجحان وقال بعضهم بأن الطرف الراجح يكون أولى ولا ينتهى إلى حد الوجوب وهو اختيار « محمود الملاى ، وأنكر بعضهم كون الأولوية كافية لمثل مامر في خواص الممكن ، وأبو الحسين وأصحابه قالوا عند الداعى يجب الفعل وعند عدمه ممتنع وذلك لا ينافي الاختيار ، فإن تفسير الاختيار هو أن يكون الفعل والترك بالقياس إلى القدرة متساويين وبالقياس إلى الداعي وعدمه إما واجب أو ممتنع ، و من عدم التمييز بين الأمرين فى هذه المسألة يحدث الاختلاف الجارى بين القائلين بالإيجاب والاختيار. ( ١٤ - المحصل )
And among them is power (qudra), and its reference in our context: if it is to soundness of limbs, it is intelligible; if it is to something beyond that, there is dispute in it. Our companions (Ash'aris) argued that the movement of the free agent (mukhtar) is distinguished from the movement of the trembling person (murtacish), and the distinction is only by this attribute. So it is said to them: When is this distinction established? Before being qualified with the act or at the time of qualification? The first is false according to your own statement, because you do not affirm power before the act. The second is likewise false, because just as the trembling person cannot stop the movement during its existence, so the free agent also cannot stop it during its existence, because it is impossible for a thing to be nonexistent and existent in one time. And it is also said: When is this distinction established? At the time God creates the movement or before it? The first is false because the occurrence of the act at the time God creates it is necessary; the second is false because its occurrence before God creates it is impossible. On both suppositions, choice is not established. (1) And it is said to the Mu'tazila: When is this choice established? When motives are equal or when one of them outweighs the other? The first is false, because when they are equal the act is impossible, and when impossible capability is not established. The second is false, because when the preponderant motive occurs, the preponderant becomes necessary and the outweighed impossible, and on this supposition we do not establish capability. (2)
(1) I say: His statement 'the free agent cannot move during the existence of movement' is questionable, because the free agent cannot move under the assumption of movement's existence, but disregarding that, why should it not be possible? Likewise for the second objection: choice at the time God creates movement is impossible under the assumption of movement's existence, but disregarding that, it is possible due to the existence of power that necessitates it. [الجائع M ولعله]

And their early scholars allowed the performance of one of the two sides by the free agent without preponderance of one over the other, and they gave examples: the thirsty person and the fleeing person when two equal loaves are present to them, and two equal times, and two equal paths; they choose one without preponderance. Those who do not allow that say: preponderance is one thing and knowledge of preponderance is another; they choose one due to the existence of preponderance even if they are not aware of it. Their later scholars said that preponderance is necessary, and some said that the preponderant side is more fitting without reaching necessity—this is the choice of Mahmud al-Mulla'. And some denied that sufficiency of priority for such cases in the properties of the possible. Abu al-Husayn and his companions said: with the motive, the act is necessary, and without it impossible, and that does not negate choice. For the interpretation of choice is that the act and its leaving are equal in relation to power, and in relation to the motive and its absence are either necessary or impossible. And from the failure to distinguish between the two matters in this question arises the ongoing disagreement between those who affirm necessitation and those who affirm choice. (14 - al-Muhassal)

Proof that Power is Simultaneous with the Act
Razi proves that power (qudra) is concurrent with the act, not prior to it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari proof that divine power (qudra) is concurrent with the act, not prior to it, and how this refutes the Mu'tazili position.

Core Idea
Power is an accident that does not endure, so it cannot exist before the act.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi presents a crisp syllogistic argument that power (qudra) must be simultaneous with the act. He stakes his claim against the Mu'tazila, who held that power pre-exists the act. The proof rests on two Ash'ari axioms: power is an accident, and accidents do not endure. Let's unpack the logic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the concise style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the premises
Find the two premises: (1) power is an accident, (2) accidents do not endure.
3
Understand the conclusion
If power preceded the act, it would vanish before the act, making the act impossible. Therefore, power must be simultaneous.
This is a simple syllogism.
4
Contrast with Mu'tazila
Recognize that the Mu'tazila hold power pre-exists, allowing choice. Ash'aris deny this due to non-endurance of accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : القدرة مع الفعل خلافا للمعتزلة) لنا أن القدرة عرض فلا تكون باقية ، فلو تقدمت على الفعل لاستحال أن يكون قادرا على الفعل
Question: Power is with the act, contrary to the Mu'tazila. Our proof: power is an accident, so it does not endure; if it preceded the act, it would be impossible for one to be capable of the act.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof that power (قدرة) is simultaneous with the act (فعل), contrary to the Mu'tazila who hold that power pre-exists the act. The author gives a single syllogistic argument: 1) Power is an accident (عرض). 2) Accidents do not endure (لا تكون باقية) — a fundamental Ash'ari occasionalist principle. 3) If power preceded the act, it would have ceased to exist before the act occurred, so the agent would not possess power at the moment of the act, making the act impossible. Therefore, power must exist at the same time as the act. The conclusion is that one is 'capable' only at the very moment the act is performed. This refutes the Mu'tazili position that power exists before the act and the agent chooses to act using that pre-existing power.

Key Terms
قدرة
عرض
باقية
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila hold that power pre-exists the act, allowing the agent to choose based on pre-existing capacity.
Correction: Ash'aris argue that since power is an accident and accidents do not endure, power cannot precede the act; it must be created concurrently with the act.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that power cannot precede the act?
A) Because power is an accident and accidents do not endure.
B) Because God creates power at the moment of the act.
C) Because the act is impossible without power.
D) Because the Mu'tazila are wrong.

Key Takeaway
Power (qudra) is an accident that does not endure, so it cannot precede the act; it exists only at the moment of the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your ability to act is only given to you at the very moment of the act, what does that reveal about your reliance on Allah for even the smallest action? How does this reshape your view of your own independence?

Think of a recent sin you committed. Did you believe you had the power to avoid it beforehand? How does this lesson challenge that belief and what does it demand from you in seeking Allah's help moment by moment?
?
When you pray, do you assume you have the power to pray properly before you begin? How can you train yourself to recognize that each word and movement depends on Allah's immediate creation of power?

Refuting Definitions of Pleasure and Pain
Al-Razi refutes definitions of pleasure and pain by Muhammad ibn Zakariyya, Ibn Sina, and the Mu'tazilites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi rejects the views that pleasure is merely relief from pain and that pain is merely perception of the disagreeable.

Core Idea
Pain is existential; pleasure is not merely the absence of pain.

Al-Razi begins by affirming that pain is an existential reality, then refutes several philosophers who tried to reduce pleasure and pain to mere perceptions. He uses a clear example to show that pleasure can arise without prior pain, thus dismantling Muhammad ibn Zakariyya's definition. He then critiques Ibn Sina and the Mu'tazilites for failing to prove that pain is nothing but perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on definitions
Carefully read the first part of the source where al-Razi presents and critiques definitions of pleasure and pain.
Focus on the example of seeing a beautiful image.
2
Identify al-Razi's objection to Muhammad ibn Zakariyya
Al-Razi uses a counterexample: viewing a beautiful image produces pleasure without prior pain. How does this refute the definition?
This shows pleasure can be positive, not just relief.
3
Analyze the critique of Ibn Sina and Mu'tazilites
Notice that al-Razi does not entirely reject their definition but says it does not prove certainty that pain is only perception.
He points out the lack of certainty, not an outright falsehood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الألم واللذة أما الألم فلا نزاع في كونه وجوديا ثم قال محمد بن زكريا اللذة عبارة عن الخلاص عن الألم وهو باطل بما إذا وقع بصر الإنسان على الإنسان على صورة مليحة فإنه يلتذ بإبصار ما مع أنه لم يكن له شعور بتلك الصورة قبل ذلك حتى تجعل تلك اللذة خلاصا عن ألم الشوق إليها . وزعم ابن سينا أن اللذة: إدراك المرافق والألم إدراك المنافي ويقرب قول المعتزلة منه فإنهم قالوا إن المدرك إن كان متعلق الشهوة كالحكة في حق الأجرب كان إدراكه لذة وإن كان متعلق النفرة كما فى حق السليم كان إدراكه ألما ومثل هذا الكلام لا يفيد القطع بأن الألم ليس إلا الإدراك واتفقت الفلاسفة على أن تفرق الاتصال موجب للألم في حق الحى وخالفتهم لأن التفرق عدمى فلا يكون علة للأمر الوجودى وزاد ابن سينا سببا ثانيا وهو سوء المزاج قال لأن حد الألم إدراك المنافى وا لحد ينعكس وكل إدراك المنافى ألم وهذه الحجة لفظية لأنه أخذ من العكس . أما قوله « التفرق عدمى فلا يكون علة للوجودى ففيه نظر لأن العدم لا يكون علة الموجود والعدمى ربما يكون علة كعدم الحركة فيما من شأنه أن يتحرك فإنه علة لأحد الأكوان الذى هو السكون وعدم السمع علة للخرس وعدم الغذاء في الحيوان السحيح للجوع وتفرق الانعال في العضو الذي لا يكون فيه أو عرص له خدر أو يكون معه استمرارا ويكون التفرق طبيعيا كما يحصل في المنتدى عند نفوذ الغذاء في أجزائه لا يكون مؤلما بل الألم عندهم إحساس عضو بتفرق اتصال يحدث فيه غير طبيعي وكلامهم يدل على ذلك ولا شك في أن الحمى وهر سوء المزاج مؤلم وإن لم يكن هناك تفرق اتصال والمعنى الجامع هو الإحساس بالمنافى فهو إذا حد الألم وإذا كان التحديد صحيحا فلا يكون انعكاسه لفظيا
And among them are pain and pleasure. As for pain, there is no dispute that it is existent. Then Muhammad ibn Zakariyya said: pleasure is deliverance from pain. This is invalid because if a person's sight falls on a beautiful image, he delights in seeing it, although he had no awareness of that image before, so that pleasure is made a deliverance from the pain of longing for it. Ibn Sina claimed that pleasure is perception of the agreeable and pain is perception of the disagreeable. The Mu'tazilites' view is close to his, for they said: if the perceived object is related to desire, like scratching for the mangy, then its perception is pleasure; and if it is related to aversion, as for the healthy, then its perception is pain. Such speech does not yield certainty that pain is nothing but perception. The philosophers agreed that separation of continuity causes pain in the living being, and I disagreed with them, because separation is non-existential, so it cannot be a cause of an existential thing. Ibn Sina added a second cause, namely bad temperament, for he said: the definition of pain is perception of the disagreeable, and the definition reverses (i.e., every perception of the disagreeable is pain). This argument is verbal because he took it from the converse. As for his saying 'separation is non-existential, so it cannot be a cause of the existent', there is consideration in it, because non-existence can be a cause, like absence of motion being a cause for rest, absence of hearing for dumbness, absence of food for hunger. Separation of continuity in a limb that is numb or where it is natural, as occurs in a sprout when food penetrates its parts, is not painful. Rather, according to them, pain is a member's sensation of an unnatural separation of continuity. Their own words indicate this. There is no doubt that fever and bad temperament are painful even if there is no separation of continuity. The unifying meaning is the sensation of the disagreeable; that is the definition of pain. If the definition is correct, its converse is not merely verbal.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical and theological discussion on pain and pleasure. Al-Razi first affirms pain's existential reality, refuting Muhammad ibn Zakariyya's reduction of pleasure to mere relief. Then he critiques Ibn Sina's and the Mu'tazilites' identification of pain with perception of the disagreeable, arguing that this does not prove pain is nothing but perception. He then rejects the philosophers' claim that separation of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisal) causes pain, because separation is non-existential and cannot cause an existential effect. He also criticizes Ibn Sina's addition of bad temperament (su' al-mizaj) as a second cause, calling it a verbal argument. Finally, al-Razi presents his own view: non-existence can be a cause in some cases, but separation is not inherently painful; pain is the sensation of unnatural separation, and the common definition of pain as perception of the disagreeable is correct, so the converse is valid only if the definition is accurate.

Key Terms
ألم
لذة
إدراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pleasure is merely the absence of pain.
Correction: Al-Razi shows pleasure can be a positive experience without prior pain, using the example of seeing a beautiful image for the first time.

Checkpoint
What example does al-Razi use to refute Muhammad ibn Zakariyya's definition of pleasure?
A) Scratching for the mangy
B) Seeing a beautiful image
C) Fever from bad temperament
D) Separation of continuity

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the reduction of pleasure to mere relief and casts doubt on the purely perceptual definition of pain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience physical pain, do you tend to think of it as merely a mental state or as a real created thing from Allah? How would viewing pain as an existent reality change your patience and trust in Allah's wisdom?
?
Recall a moment of pure pleasure—like seeing a beautiful scene or tasting something delicious. Was that pleasure merely relief from a previous discomfort, or was it a positive gift? How can acknowledging pleasure as a created reality increase your gratitude?
?
Al-Razi challenges the view that pain is only perception. If you started treating your own pains as real existential trials, not just subjective feelings, how would that affect your dua, your sabr, and your understanding of divine decree?

Critique of Philosophers' Causation of Pain
Al-Razi critiques the philosophers' claim about separation of continuity and Ibn Sina's verbal argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Razi rejects the philosophers' claim that separation of continuity causes pain and why he considers Ibn Sina's argument verbal.

Core Idea
Non-existence cannot cause existence; verbal definitions do not prove causality.

This passage presents the philosophers' consensus that pain results from separation of continuity (like a cut). Al-Razi objects because separation is non-existential and cannot cause an existent pain. He then criticizes Ibn Sina's additional cause of bad temperament, arguing it is based on a verbal trick—confusing the definition's converse with a causal relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim
Understand that the philosophers hold that separation of continuity causes pain in living beings.
This is a common physical explanation.
2
Analyze al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi argues that separation is non-existential (it is an absence of connection), so it cannot be the cause of something existential like pain.
This is a metaphysical principle: non-being cannot produce being.
3
Examine Ibn Sina's addition
Ibn Sina adds bad temperament as a second cause, based on his definition of pain. Al-Razi says this argument is merely verbal because it uses the converse of the definition.
Just because all pains are perceptions of the disagreeable does not mean all perceptions of the disagreeable are pains? Actually he says the definition 'pain is perception of the disagreeable' reverses to 'every perception of the disagreeable is pain', but he calls it a verbal trick.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها الألم واللذة أما الألم فلا نزاع في كونه وجوديا ثم قال محمد بن زكريا اللذة عبارة عن الخلاص عن الألم وهو باطل بما إذا وقع بصر الإنسان على الإنسان على صورة مليحة فإنه يلتذ بإبصار ما مع أنه لم يكن له شعور بتلك الصورة قبل ذلك حتى تجعل تلك اللذة خلاصا عن ألم الشوق إليها . وزعم ابن سينا أن اللذة: إدراك المرافق والألم إدراك المنافي ويقرب قول المعتزلة منه فإنهم قالوا إن المدرك إن كان متعلق الشهوة كالحكة في حق الأجرب كان إدراكه لذة وإن كان متعلق النفرة كما فى حق السليم كان إدراكه ألما ومثل هذا الكلام لا يفيد القطع بأن الألم ليس إلا الإدراك واتفقت الفلاسفة على أن تفرق الاتصال موجب للألم في حق الحى وخالفتهم لأن التفرق عدمى فلا يكون علة للأمر الوجودى وزاد ابن سينا سببا ثانيا وهو سوء المزاج قال لأن حد الألم إدراك المنافى وا لحد ينعكس وكل إدراك المنافى ألم وهذه الحجة لفظية لأنه أخذ من العكس . أما قوله « التفرق عدمى فلا يكون علة للوجودى ففيه نظر لأن العدم لا يكون علة الموجود والعدمى ربما يكون علة كعدم الحركة فيما من شأنه أن يتحرك فإنه علة لأحد الأكوان الذى هو السكون وعدم السمع علة للخرس وعدم الغذاء في الحيوان السحيح للجوع وتفرق الانعال في العضو الذي لا يكون فيه أو عرص له خدر أو يكون معه استمرارا ويكون التفرق طبيعيا كما يحصل في المنتدى عند نفوذ الغذاء في أجزائه لا يكون مؤلما بل الألم عندهم إحساس عضو بتفرق اتصال يحدث فيه غير طبيعي وكلامهم يدل على ذلك ولا شك في أن الحمى وهر سوء المزاج مؤلم وإن لم يكن هناك تفرق اتصال والمعنى الجامع هو الإحساس بالمنافى فهو إذا حد الألم وإذا كان التحديد صحيحا فلا يكون انعكاسه لفظيا
And among them are pain and pleasure. As for pain, there is no dispute that it is existent. Then Muhammad ibn Zakariyya said: pleasure is deliverance from pain. This is invalid because if a person's sight falls on a beautiful image, he delights in seeing it, although he had no awareness of that image before, so that pleasure is made a deliverance from the pain of longing for it. Ibn Sina claimed that pleasure is perception of the agreeable and pain is perception of the disagreeable. The Mu'tazilites' view is close to his, for they said: if the perceived object is related to desire, like scratching for the mangy, then its perception is pleasure; and if it is related to aversion, as for the healthy, then its perception is pain. Such speech does not yield certainty that pain is nothing but perception. The philosophers agreed that separation of continuity causes pain in the living being, and I disagreed with them, because separation is non-existential, so it cannot be a cause of an existential thing. Ibn Sina added a second cause, namely bad temperament, for he said: the definition of pain is perception of the disagreeable, and the definition reverses (i.e., every perception of the disagreeable is pain). This argument is verbal because he took it from the converse. As for his saying 'separation is non-existential, so it cannot be a cause of the existent', there is consideration in it, because non-existence can be a cause, like absence of motion being a cause for rest, absence of hearing for dumbness, absence of food for hunger. Separation of continuity in a limb that is numb or where it is natural, as occurs in a sprout when food penetrates its parts, is not painful. Rather, according to them, pain is a member's sensation of an unnatural separation of continuity. Their own words indicate this. There is no doubt that fever and bad temperament are painful even if there is no separation of continuity. The unifying meaning is the sensation of the disagreeable; that is the definition of pain. If the definition is correct, its converse is not merely verbal.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical and theological discussion on pain and pleasure. Al-Razi first affirms pain's existential reality, refuting Muhammad ibn Zakariyya's reduction of pleasure to mere relief. Then he critiques Ibn Sina's and the Mu'tazilites' identification of pain with perception of the disagreeable, arguing that this does not prove pain is nothing but perception. He then rejects the philosophers' claim that separation of continuity (tafarruq al-ittisal) causes pain, because separation is non-existential and cannot cause an existential effect. He also criticizes Ibn Sina's addition of bad temperament (su' al-mizaj) as a second cause, calling it a verbal argument. Finally, al-Razi presents his own view: non-existence can be a cause in some cases, but separation is not inherently painful; pain is the sensation of unnatural separation, and the common definition of pain as perception of the disagreeable is correct, so the converse is valid only if the definition is accurate.

Misconception: Separation of continuity is a physical cause of pain.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that separation is non-existential and cannot cause existential pain.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Razi reject the philosophers' view that separation of continuity causes pain?
A) Because separation is non-existential and cannot cause an existential effect.
B) Because pain is not caused by physical events.
C) Because the philosophers did not define pain.
D) Because separation actually causes pleasure.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi refutes the causal claim of philosophers based on the metaphysical principle that non-existence cannot cause existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief about why something happens simply because it was defined that way, without examining the underlying cause? Where in your own thinking might you be relying on verbal definitions rather than true causality?
?
Al-Razi refused to attribute an existential effect (pain) to a non-existential cause (separation). In your life, do you sometimes blame 'absences' (lack of money, time, or opportunity) for your problems when the real cause may lie elsewhere? What would it cost you to honestly investigate that?
?
Ibn Sina's argument was dismissed as a mere verbal trick. What arguments or justifications do you use that might be just clever words without substance? Will you have the courage to expose them and rebuild your understanding from the ground up?

Non-Existential Causes and the Correct Definition of Pain
Al-Razi acknowledges non-existential causes, qualifies separation as not always painful, and settles on the definition of pain as sensation of the disagreeable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that non-existence can sometimes be a cause, but separation of continuity is not always painful, and that pain is correctly defined as sensation of the disagreeable.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be a cause, but pain is ultimately the sensation of the disagreeable.

Al-Razi now offers a more nuanced view. He concedes that non-existence can indeed be a cause in some cases, but he also shows that separation of continuity is not always painful—for instance, in numb limbs or natural processes like growth. He then argues that the true definition of pain is the sensation of the disagreeable, and if that definition is correct, its converse is valid, contrary to his earlier dismissal.

Guided Path
1
Read the nuanced argument
Al-Razi begins by acknowledging that non-existence can be a cause, giving examples like absence of motion causing rest.
This qualifies his earlier blanket rejection.
2
Examine the exceptions to separation causing pain
He notes that separation is not painful in numb limbs or in natural processes like growth. This shows separation is not the sole cause.
This is an empirical observation.
3
Understand the final definition
Al-Razi concludes that pain is sensation of the disagreeable, and that if this definition is correct, the converse holds—contra his earlier charge of verbal trick.
He seems to accept Ibn Sina's definition but now considers the converse valid if the definition is accurate.

?

Refutation of Extramission and Intromission Theories of Sight
Razi refutes the extramission and intromission theories of vision, with a digression on causation and pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical refutations of the two major theories of vision (extramission and intromission) and the philosophical issues of causation and pain that arise in the debate.

Core Idea
Sight is not explained by a ray from the eye nor by an impression of the object; both theories face deep problems.

This passage from Imam al-Razi presents a careful critique of two ancient theories of vision. He first states the standard objections against each, then addresses a nuanced objection about non-existence as a cause, and finally offers a deeper analysis showing the strengths and weaknesses of both positions. By the end, you will see that the debate is more subtle than first appearances suggest.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two theories
Read the first two sentences to find the extramission and intromission theories and their initial refutations.
Notice that each refutation is a single argument.
2
Examine the digression on causation
Read the paragraph starting with 'أما قوله' to understand how non-existence can be a cause and how pain is defined.
This section clarifies an important logical principle.
3
Follow the deeper analysis
Read the 'أقول' section to see the author's defense of both theories and his evaluation of their arguments.
Pay attention to how he uses analogies and specific points.
4
Synthesize the overall argument
Consider how the initial refutations and the deeper analysis together form a comprehensive critique. Ask yourself: does the author endorse either theory?
The answer is no; he shows both are problematic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منهم من قال إنه بخروج الشعاع عن العين وهو باطل، وإلا لوجب تشوش الأبصار عند هبوب الرياح، ولا متنع أن يرى نصف السماء لامتناع أن يخرج من حدقتنا ما يتصل بكل هذه الأشياء أو يؤثر في جميع الأجسام. ومنهم من قال بالانطباع وهو باطل، وإلا لما أدركنا العظيم لامتناع انطباع العظيم في الصغير.
أما قوله «التفرق عدمي فلا يكون علة للوجودي»، ففيه نظر، لأن العدم لا يكون علة الموجود، والعدمي ربما يكون علة، كعدم الحركة فيما من شأنه أن يتحرك فإنه علة لأحد الأكوان الذي هو السكون، وعدم السمع علة للخرس، وعدم الغذاء في الحيوان السحيح للجوع. وتفرق الاتصال في العضو الذي لا يكون فيه ألم أو عرص له خدر أو يكون معه استمرارا، ويكون التفرق طبيعيا كما يحصل في المنتدى عند نفوذ الغذاء في أجزائه، لا يكون مؤلما، بل الألم عندهم إحساس عضو بتفرق اتصال يحدث فيه غير طبيعي. وكلامهم يدل على ذلك، ولا شك في أن الحمى وسوء المزاج مؤلم وإن لم يكن هناك تفرق اتصال. والمعنى الجامع هو الإحساس بالمنافي فهو إذا حد الألم. وإذا كان التحديد صحيحا فلا يكون انعكاسه لفظيا.
أقول: القائلون بالشعاع وهم الحكماء المتقدمون لا يقولون بخروجه عن العين إلا بالمجاز كما يقال الضوء مخرج من الشمس، وإبطاله بوجوب تشوشه عند هبوب الرياح ليس بوارد، لأن شعاع الشمس والقمر والنيرات لا يتشوش به. وأيضا قالوا لو كان الشعاع جسما لزم تداخل الأجسام، ولو كان عرضا لزم انتقال الأعراض. وأيضا قالوا إن الشعاع من العين كيف يصل إلى السماء دفعة، فإن الحركة محتاجة إلى الزمان وغير ذلك. وكل ذلك لازم على سائر الأشعة، وكل ما يقولون في جوابه هناك هو الجواب ههنا. وامتناع رؤية نصف السماء بشعاع الحدقة دعوى مجردة، ولو قال بدل الامتناع الاستبعاد لكان أصوب. وإذا جاز نور سراج صغير أن يضيء هواء بيت كبير وجدرانه ولم يستبعد ذلك، فذلك أيضا ليس بمستبعد. واستدلوا على كون الإبصار بالشعاع باشتراطه كون المبصر في ضوء، ولولا أن شعاع البصر والضوء من جنس واحد لما كان بعضه معينا في إفادة البعض. وأيضا كما يقع لشعاع الأجرام النيرة انعكاس وانعطاف ونفوذ فيما يحاذيها من الأجسام الشفافة يقع لشعاع العين مثله بعينه كما تبين في الصغير. ولما رأينا القريب على قربه والبعيد على بعده، فهذان الوجهان إنما يلزمان من قال المرئي هذه المنطبعة جعل انطباع الصورة الصغيرة في الحدقة شرطا لإدراك المرئي الكبير في الخارج لا يرد عليه ذلك فقط.
وأما من قال بالانطباع: إنما قال به أرسطاطاليس وأصحابه وبينوا السبب في رؤية العظيم من بعيد صغيرا. وإبطاله بامتناع انطباع العظيم في الصغير غير صحيح، لأنهم لا يشترطون فيه انطباع العظيم نفسه أو مقداره، بل قالوا بانطباع شبح منه، ولعل مقدار الشبح على صغر محله يقتضي إدراك ذي الشبح على عظمه، وذلك كما ينطبع في المرآة نصف السماء والأجرام التي فيه. وأما رؤية القريب على قربه والبعيد على بعده يعني الأبعاد، فلعل المنطبع في العين يكون على هيئة تفيد إدراك الأبعاد. ونحن لما تعذر علينا أن نعبر عنه استبعدناه، مع أنا نرى النقاشين ينقشون صور الأجسام على السطوح على وجه يدرك الناظر فيها أعماق تلك الأجسام وأبعاد ما بينها.
Among them are those who said that it [sight] is by the emission of a ray from the eye, and it is false; otherwise, sight would necessarily be disturbed at the blowing of winds, and it would be impossible to see half the sky because it is impossible that something emerges from our pupil that connects to all these things or affects all bodies. And among them are those who said by impression (intiṭbāʿ), and it is false; otherwise we would not perceive the large because of the impossibility of the large impressing in the small.
As for his statement: 'Discontinuity (tafarruq) is non-existent, so it cannot be a cause of the existent', there is consideration in it, because non-existence cannot be a cause of the existent, and non-existent perhaps can be a cause, like the absence of motion in that which is disposed to move, for it is a cause of one of the states (akwān) which is rest, and the absence of hearing is a cause of deafness, and the absence of nourishment in a starving animal is a cause of hunger. And the discontinuity of connection in a limb in which there is no pain or numbness or with which there is continuity, and the discontinuity is natural as occurs in the growing body when nourishment penetrates its parts, is not painful. Rather, pain according to them is a limb's sensation of a discontinuity of connection that occurs in it unnaturally. Their speech indicates that, and there is no doubt that fever and poor temperament are painful even if there is no discontinuity of connection. And the comprehensive meaning is the sensation of the harmful, so that is the definition of pain. And if the definition is correct, then its reverse is not verbal.
I say: Those who advocate the ray, who are the ancient philosophers, do not say that it [the ray] emerges from the eye except metaphorically, as it is said that light emerges from the sun; and its refutation by the necessity of its disturbance at the blowing of winds is not valid, because the ray of the sun, moon, and luminous bodies is not disturbed by it. And also they said: if the ray were a body, then the interpenetration of bodies would be necessary; and if it were an accident, then the transfer of accidents would be necessary. And also they said: how does the ray from the eye reach the sky instantaneously, for motion requires time, etc. And all that is necessary for other rays, and whatever they say in response to it there is the response here. And the impossibility of seeing half the sky by the ray of the pupil is a mere claim; and if instead of impossibility he said improbability, it would be more correct. And if a small lamp's light can illuminate the air of a large room and its walls and that is not deemed improbable, then this also is not improbable. And they argued for vision being by the ray by the condition that the seen object be in light; and were it not that the ray of sight and light are of the same kind, then one would not be helpful in producing the other. And also, just as for the ray of luminous bodies occur reflection, refraction, and penetration into the transparent bodies facing them, the same occurs for the ray of the eye, as is shown in the small. And since we see the near at its nearness and the far at its distance, these two aspects only follow from those who say that the seen is this impression [in the eye], making the impression of the small image in the pupil a condition for perceiving the large seen object externally; it is not refuted by that alone.
As for those who advocate impression (al-intibāʿ), Aristotle and his followers said it and they explained the reason for seeing the large from a distance as small. And its refutation by the impossibility of the large impressing in the small is not correct, because they do not require the impression of the large itself or its size, but rather they say by the impression of a phantom (shabaḥ) from it; perhaps the measure of the phantom, given the smallness of its location, necessitates perceiving the possessor of the phantom as large, just as half the sky and the celestial bodies in it impress in a mirror. And as for seeing the near at its nearness and the far at its distance, i.e., distances, perhaps the impressed [form] in the eye is of a form that conveys perception of distances. And since we found it difficult to express it, we deemed it improbable, even though we see painters painting images of bodies on surfaces in such a way that the observer perceives the depths of those bodies and the distances between them.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a refutation and discussion of two classical theories of vision: the extramission theory (vision by emission of a ray from the eye) and the intromission theory (vision by impression of an image in the eye). The author first refutes extramission: if sight were by a ray, then wind would disturb it, and it would be impossible to see half the sky at once. He then refutes intromission: if it were by impression, then large objects could not be perceived because a large image cannot fit in the small eye. A digression addresses an objection about non-existence as a cause, arguing that nonexistence can indeed be a cause (e.g., absence of motion causes rest, absence of food causes hunger). Similarly, the sensation of pain is defined as the sensation of harm, not necessarily of discontinuity. The commentator then elaborates on the extramission theory, showing that ancient philosophers did not hold it literally, and that the refutations are weak. He also defends the intromission theory: the impression is of a phantom (shabaḥ) not the actual size; the perception of distance is explained by the form of the impression. Both theories are discussed critically, but the author does not definitively endorse either, noting the complexity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that proving the impossibility of one theory directly proves the other; the passage shows both are problematic and require careful handling.
Correction: The passage does not endorse either theory; it critiques both and shows that each has strengths and weaknesses.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the refutation of the extramission theory (al-shuʿāʿ) regarding seeing half the sky?
A) The ray would be disturbed by wind.
B) The ray cannot reach the sky instantaneously.
C) It is impossible for the ray to encompass half the sky.
D) The ray would cause interpenetration of bodies.

Key Takeaway
The classical theories of vision are not easily defeated; each has strengths and weaknesses that require nuanced philosophical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a strong opinion about a matter, do you genuinely examine the strongest arguments of the opposing view, or do you dismiss them quickly? This lesson shows that even flawed theories can have valid points. What is one belief you hold that you have not critically examined from the other side, and what step will you take this week to understand it better?
?
Razi defines pain as the sensation of harm (al-īḥsās bil-munāfī). Think of a recent pain you experienced—physical or emotional. How might redefining it as a sensation of something harmful to you change your response to it? Are there ‘harms’ you endure that you have normalized and stopped feeling as pain? What would it mean to become sensitive to them again?
?
The passage discusses how absence (non-existence) can be a cause—like lack of food causes hunger. In your spiritual life, what absences—such as lack of gratitude, prayer, or sincere intention—might be causing negative states in your heart? Identify one absence and commit to a specific action to replace it with presence.

Perception is Not Necessitated by Conditions
Razi argues against the necessity of perception given conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari position holds that perception is not logically necessary even when all conditions are met, and how Razi supports this with two proofs and a refutation of the Mu'tazilite objection.

Core Idea
Sense perception is habitual, not necessary.

Imam Razi presents a distinctive Ash'ari argument: having a sound sense organ and a visible object does not logically force perception to occur. He provides two compelling proofs from ordinary experience and addresses the objection that this would lead to absurd possibilities. The key is that we rely on custom (ʿādāt), not necessity, for regular perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to grasp the overall argument structure.
Notice the strong opening: 'غير واجب عندنا' — this is a stance against the Mu'tazilites and philosophers.
2
Identify the two Ash'ari proofs
The passage gives two proofs. Find them and understand how each demonstrates that perception is not necessitated by conditions.
The first proof uses the example of seeing a large object as small from a distance.
3
Examine the Mu'tazilite objection and the Ash'ari answer
Read the objection and Razi's response. Why does Razi dismiss the objection by appealing to 'habitual occurrences'?
The answer is concise: 'يُعترض بجميع العاديات' — the objection applies to all habitual events, not just perception.
4
Reflect on the concept of habit (ʿāda)
Consider how this argument fits into the broader Ash'ari rejection of necessary causal connections. Perception is one domain where we rely on Allah's custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الإدراك عند سلامة الحاسة وحصول المبصر وسائر الشرائط المشهورة غير واجب عندنا خلافا للمعتزلة والفلاسفة. لأنا نرى الكبير من البعيد صغيرا، وما ذاك إلا لأنا نرى بعض أجزائه دون البعض مع استوائها بأثرها في كل الشرائط. ولأنا لما رأينا الجسم الكبير فقد رأينا كل واحد من تلك الأجزاء، وليست رؤية كل جزء مشروطة برؤية الجزء الآخر وإلا وقع الدور، فرؤية كل واحد منها غنية عن رؤية الآخر. واحتجوا بأنه لولا ذلك لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا شموس وجبال ونحن لا نراها. الجواب: أنه معارض بجميع العاديات.
Issue: Perception with the soundness of the sense, the presence of the visible object, and all the other well-known conditions is not necessary according to us, contrary to the Mu'tazilites and philosophers. For we see the large from a distance as small, and that is only because we see some of its parts without others, despite their equality in all conditions. And because when we see the large body, we have seen each one of those parts, and the vision of each part is not conditioned by the vision of the other part, otherwise circularity would occur, so the vision of each one is independent of the vision of the other. And they argued that if it were not necessary, then it would be possible that there are suns and mountains before us and we do not see them. The answer: it is countered by all habitual occurrences (al-ʿādiyāt).
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents a distinctive Ash'ari position: perception (al-idrāk) is not logically or causally necessitated by the presence of a sound sense organ, the visible object, and all other conditions. The author gives two proofs. First, we see a large object from afar as small because we actually see only some of its parts, not all, even though all parts have equal access to the sense. This shows that the same conditions yield different results, so conditions do not necessitate perception. Second, when we see a large body, we see each of its parts, but the vision of each part does not depend on the vision of the others (otherwise there would be circularity). Therefore, perception of each part is self-sufficient, and the conditions are not the sole cause. The opposing argument—that if perception were not necessitated, we might fail to see something present—is refuted by appealing to 'habitual occurrences' (al-ʿādiyāt): we rely on custom, not logical necessity, to expect vision when conditions are met.

Key Terms
الإدراك
سلامة الحاسة
الشرائط
الواجب
المعتزلة
الفلاسفة
العاديات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sense perception is automatically produced when the sense organ is sound, the object is present, and conditions are normal.
Correction: The passage argues that perception is not necessitated by these conditions; it is only habitual. The same conditions sometimes yield different results (e.g., seeing a large object as small).

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that perception is not necessary even when conditions are fulfilled?
A) Because sense organs are fallible.
B) Because we see large objects as small from a distance.
C) Because the existence of conditions guarantees perception.
D) Because the Mu'tazilites agree.

Key Takeaway
Perception is not a necessary outcome of its conditions; it is a habitual occurrence dependent on Allah's custom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at a loved one or a beautiful scene, do you assume that your perception is automatic and guaranteed? How would your gratitude change if you realized that each moment of seeing is a fresh act of Allah’s custom, not a mechanical necessity?
?
Think of a time you were deceived by appearances—someone seemed trustworthy but wasn't, or a situation looked harmless but harmed you. How does Razi's argument that perception is not necessary challenge your tendency to trust your senses blindly? Will you now pause before relying on what you see?
?
If perception is merely habitual, then your entire experience of the world is a series of divine habits. Are you living as though the world runs on its own, or as though every sight, sound, and feeling is directly sustained by Allah? What would change in your daily awareness if you truly internalized this?

Hearing Does Not Require Air Reaching the Ear
Razi refutes the necessity of air reaching the ear for hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash'ari theologians deny that hearing depends on air carrying sound physically reaching the ear.

Core Idea
Hearing is a direct creation of God, not a physical process.

This short but powerful passage sets Ash'ari occasionalism against the physical causality of the philosophers and Mu'tazila. Imam Razi presents a simple counterexample that challenges the assumption that sound requires physical transmission. As you read, notice how the argument works: it points to an everyday experience that would be impossible if the physical theory were true.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Let the author's own argument come through before analyzing.
Notice how concise the argument is.
2
Identify the disagreement
The text states that the Ash'ari position is that hearing does not require air reaching the ear, contrary to the philosophers and al-Naẓẓām. Understand the opposing view.
Al-Naẓẓām was a Mu'tazilite who held a physical theory of sound.
3
Analyze the proof
The proof is a counterexample: if hearing required air carrying sound to reach the ear, we could not hear someone moving between us and the speaker. Since we do hear such speech, the condition is not necessary.
This argument relies on the assumption that moving would disrupt the air path.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: اختلفوا في أنه هل يعتبر في السمع وصول الهواء الحامل للصوت إلى الصماخ؟ فعندنا أنه غير واجب خلافا للفلاسفة والنظام. لنا أنه لو كان كما قالوا لما سمعنا كلام من يجول بيننا وبينه.
Issue: They differed concerning whether for hearing the arrival of the air carrying the sound to the ear canal is necessary. According to us it is not necessary, contrary to the philosophers and al-Naẓẓām. For us, if it were as they say, then we would not hear the speech of one who moves about between us and him.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage addresses a specific physical question: does hearing require that the air bearing sound waves reach the ear canal? The author, following the Ash'ari school, says it is not necessary, rejecting the views of the philosophers and al-Naẓẓām (a Mu'tazilite). The proof is a counterexample: if hearing were solely dependent on air reaching the ear, then we could not hear someone who is moving around (i.e., changing location) while between us and the speaker? Alternatively, the phrase 'من يجول بيننا وبينه' might mean 'someone who moves about between us and him,' suggesting that the sound would be interrupted. The point is that we do hear such speech, so the condition of air arrival is not necessary. This aligns with the occasionalist Ash'ari view that God creates hearing directly, not through physical causality.

Key Terms
السمع
الهواء
الصوت
الصماخ
النظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think hearing requires air carrying sound to reach the ear as a necessary physical condition.
Correction: The Ash'ari argument shows this is not necessary, as we hear speech even when the air path is disrupted by movement.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the counterexample to the claim that hearing requires air reaching the ear?
A) We can hear in a vacuum.
B) We hear the speech of someone moving between us and the speaker.
C) We can hear sounds from far away.
D) Sound travels through solids.

Key Takeaway
Hearing does not depend on physical transmission; God creates hearing directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear a sound, do you instantly recognize that God directly creates that hearing, or do you take it for granted as a natural process? How would your awareness of His constant creation change your gratitude?
?
The argument in this passage challenges your reliance on physical explanations. In what area of your life do you assume things must happen through material causes, and how can you begin to trust God's direct power more?
?
If hearing does not depend on air reaching the ear, then God can make you hear even when physical conditions are impossible. Does this truth make you more expectant of His help in situations that seem hopeless? How will you act on that expectation today?

Two Objections to the Soul's Attachment and Their Answers
Razi poses and answers two objections to the Jermanian theory of the soul's attachment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Jermanian theologians defended the soul's attachment to matter against objections about its cause and why God permitted it.

Core Idea
The soul's attachment is defended as either a free choice or an eternal causal chain, and allowed by God for the soul's ultimate benefit.

Imam Razi presents two objections that challenge the Jermanian theory of the soul's attachment to matter. The first objection questions the cause of the attachment at a specific moment; the second asks why God did not prevent it. Razi then records the Jermanians' answers, which appeal to different principles for different audiences—theologians and philosophers. This lesson unpacks these responses, showing how they aim to reconcile the soul's pre-existence with divine wisdom and causality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two questions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the two distinct objections raised against the Jermanian theory.
The first objection concerns the cause of attachment; the second concerns divine permission.
2
Analyze the first answer: two approaches for two audiences
Notice how the Jermanians answer differently for the mutakallimūn (free agent can choose without cause) and the falāsifa (eternal chain of causes). Identify the reasoning for each.
This shows the Jermanians addressing both theological and philosophical frameworks.
3
Examine the second answer: God's wisdom and the soul's benefit
Understand the two reasons given: the soul learns from the pain of attachment and gains intellectual virtues. This justifies why God allowed it.
The answer emphasizes divine wisdom and the soul's ultimate good.
4
Reflect on the coherence of the Jermanian defense
Consider whether the answers are consistent. Note that they target different audiences and aspects of the problem.
The common misunderstanding is that the answers are contradictory, but they are tailored to different assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بقى ههنا سؤالان
أحدهما أن يقال : لم تعلق النفس بالهيولى بعد أن كانت غير متعلقة بها فإن حدث ذلك التعلق لا عن سبب مجوز حديث العالم بكلية لاعى سبب .
والثاني : أن يقال فهلا منع البارى تعالى النفس من التعلق بالهيولى .
أجابوا عن الأول : أن هذا السؤال غير مقبول فى المتكلمين ، لأنهم يقولون الفادر المختار قد مقدوريه على الآخر من شاير مرجح فهلا جوزوا ذلك فى النفس ؟ وغير مقبول أيضاً من الفلاسفة لأنهم جوزوا في السابق أن يكون علة اللاحق، فهلا جوزوا أن يقال النفس قديمة ولها تصويرات متحددة غير متناهية ولم يزل كل سابق علة للاسق حتى انتهت إلى ذلك النصور الموجب لذلك النعلق ؟
وأجابوا من السؤال الثاني : بأن البارى تعالى علم بأن الأصلح للنفس أن تتصور عالمها بمضاد هذا النعلق حتى انها بنفسها تمتنع من تلك المخالطة ، وأيضاً فالنفس بمخالطتها الهيولى تكسب من الفضائل العقلية ما لم يكن موجودا لها فلهذين الفرضين لم يمنع البارى النفس من التعليق بالهيولى
There remain here two questions: the first: it may be said: why did the soul attach to matter after it was not attached? For if that attachment occurred without a cause, the temporal origination of the entire world would be uncaused. The second: it may be said: why did the Creator not prevent the soul from attaching to matter? They answered the first: that this question is not acceptable to the mutakallimūn, because they say the free agent [may prefer] one of its possibilities over another without a determinant, so why do they not allow that for the soul? It is also not acceptable from the philosophers, because they allow that the prior can be the cause of the latter, so why do they not allow that it be said: the soul is eternal and has infinite successive determinations, and every prior is the cause of the subsequent until it reaches that determination which necessitates that attachment? And they answered the second question: that the Creator knew that it is more beneficial for the soul to perceive its world through the opposite of this attachment, so that it itself will refrain from that mixing. Also, through its mixing with matter, the soul acquires intellectual virtues that were not present for it. For these two reasons, the Creator did not prevent the soul from attaching to matter.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage addresses two objections to the Jermanian theory of the soul's attachment to matter. The first objection: what caused the soul to attach at a particular moment? If no cause, then the world's creation is arbitrary. The Jermanians answer that this objection is not valid. For the mutakallimūn (theologians), they allow that a free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) can choose between possibilities without a determinant, so why not admit that for the soul? For the philosophers (falāsifa), they hold that the prior can cause the posterior in an eternal chain of determinations; thus the soul's eternal successive determinations lead inevitably to the attachment. The second objection: why did God not prevent the soul's attachment? The answer: God knew that the soul's direct experience of the pain of attachment would lead it to voluntarily avoid mixing, and that by mixing it gains intellectual virtues. Therefore, He allowed it for the soul's ultimate benefit. The answers show an attempt to reconcile the Jermanian theory with both theological and philosophical frameworks.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Jermanian answers are contradictory because they appeal to different principles.
Correction: The answers are not contradictory; they are tailored to different audiences (theologians vs. philosophers) and address different aspects of the objection.

Checkpoint
How do the Jermanians answer the first objection for the philosophers?
A) They say the soul's attachment is uncaused.
B) They say the soul's eternal successive determinations lead to the attachment.
C) They say God directly caused it.
D) They say it is a mystery.

Key Takeaway
The Jermanians defended the soul's attachment by appealing to either free agency (for theologians) or causal determinism (for philosophers), and justified God's permission through the soul's ultimate benefit and acquisition of virtues.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the soul's attachment to matter is allowed by God for its ultimate benefit, how does that reshape how you view the difficulties and trials in your own life? Will you now see them as opportunities to acquire virtues you lack, and what is one specific struggle you will reframe today?
?
The passage says the Creator did not prevent attachment because He knew the soul would benefit. When have you blamed God for allowing a situation that felt purposeless? Are you ready to trust that He sees a benefit even when you do not, and to let that trust change your attitude toward a current hardship?
?
The Jermanians argued that the soul learns to refrain from mixing by experiencing the opposite. Is there a 'mixing' in your life—a habit, a relationship, a compromise—that you know harms you but you have not yet stopped? Will you allow the pain of that experience to finally teach you, or will you wait for another lesson?

The Pythagorean Doctrine: Numbers as Principles of All Things
Razi presents the Pythagorean view that numbers are the principles of all things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Pythagorean argument that numbers derived from units are the principles of all things, and how they deduce physical bodies from abstract units.

Core Idea
Numbers from units are the ultimate principles of all existence.

Imam Razi presents the Pythagorean worldview, which seeks to explain all of reality from mathematical entities. The Pythagoreans argued that composites depend on simples, and these simples must be self-subsisting units. In this passage, you will encounter their logical deduction that leads from abstract units to physical bodies.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical structure: claim, reasoning, derivation.
2
Identify the key claim
The Pythagoreans claim that the principles (mabādiʾ) are numbers generated from units. This is the foundational idea.
3
Follow the argument for units being self-subsisting
The passage presents a dichotomy: simples either have a quiddity beyond being a unit or not. If they did, they'd be composite, which is not allowed. So they are mere units, and must be independent.
This is a reductio argument.
4
Trace the derivation of bodies from units
A unit with position becomes a point; two points make a line; two lines make a surface; two surfaces make a body. See how abstract units generate physical reality.
This shows the Pythagorean claim that bodies ultimately come from units.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
للفرقة الثانية هم أصحاب فيثاغورث : وهم الذين قالوا المبادى هى الأعداد المتولدة في الوحدات ، قالوا لأن قوام المركبات بالبسائط وهمى أمور كل واحد منها فى نفسه واحد ، ثم تلك الأمور إما أن تكون لها ماهيات وراء كونها وحدات أو لا تكون ، فإن كان الأول كانت مركبة ، لأن هناك تلك الماهية مع تلك الوحدة وكلامنا ليس في المركبات بلى في مباديها وإن كان الثاني ثانت مجرد وحدات ، وهى لابد وأن تكون مستقلة بأنفسها وإلا لكانت مفقرة إلى الغير فيكون ذلك الغير أقدم منها ، وكلامنا في المبادى المطلقة هذا خلف ، فإذا الوحدات. أمور قائمة بأنفسها ، فإن عرض الوضع للوحدة صارت نقطة ، فإن اجتمعت نقطتان حصل الخط ، فإن اجتمع الخطان حصل السطح ، فإن اجتمع السطحان حصل الجسم ، فظهر أن مبدأ الاجسام الوحدات
The second group: they are the followers of Pythagoras, who said: the principles are numbers generated from units. They said: because the subsistence of composites is through simples, and these simples are things each of which is in itself one. Then those things either have quiddities beyond being units, or not. If the first, they would be composite, because there would be that quiddity along with that unity, and our discussion is not about composites but about their principles. If the second, they are mere units, and they must be independent in themselves, otherwise they would be dependent on another, making that other more prior, and our discussion is about absolute principles, which would be contradictory. So the units are self-subsisting things. If position (waḍʿ) attaches to a unit, it becomes a point. If two points meet, a line is obtained. If two lines meet, a surface is obtained. If two surfaces meet, a body is obtained. Thus it appears that the principle of bodies is units.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage defines the Pythagorean doctrine as taught by Razi. The Pythagoreans (Aṣḥāb Fīthāghūrth) hold that the principles (mabādiʾ) of all things are numbers (aʿdād) generated from units (waḥdāt). The argument proceeds: composites (murakkabāt) depend on simples (basā'iṭ). Each simple is in itself one (one unit). These simples either have an essence (māhiyya) beyond being a unit or not. If they had, they would be composite (having both unity and a quiddity), but the discussion is about simples, not composites. If they do not, they are mere units. Units must be self-subsisting (qā'ima bi-anfusihā), otherwise they would depend on something prior, contradicting their role as absolute principles. Therefore, units are the ultimate principles. When position (waḍʿ) is assigned to a unit, it becomes a point; points generate lines, lines generate surfaces, surfaces generate bodies. Hence, bodies originate from units. The passage demonstrates a logical deduction of physical reality from abstract mathematical entities, reflecting the Pythagorean worldview.

Key Terms
الأعداد
الوحدات
النقطة
الخط
السطح
الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'units' (waḥdāt) refer to physical atoms or material particles.
Correction: In this Pythagorean context, units are abstract mathematical entities, not physical atoms. They are the basis for numbers and eventually give rise to physical bodies through geometric derivation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the immediate principle of a body?
A) Numbers
B) Units
C) Surfaces
D) The four elements

Key Takeaway
The Pythagorean doctrine reduces all physical reality to units and numbers through a logical derivation from abstract units to concrete bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you normally think of physical objects as being ultimately made of material particles? This passage suggests they derive from abstract units. Does that challenge your assumption? Why?
?
If the ultimate principles are not material but mathematical, how does that affect your understanding of the world's reality? Are you comforted or unsettled by the idea that a rock is, at its core, a number?
?
The argument relies on the necessity of simples being self-subsisting. Consider your own soul: do you consider it simple and self-subsisting? Why or why not, and what does that imply about its relationship to the physical body?
