Eternal Motion and the Principle of Priority
Objection and response about eternal motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the world could have been moving eternally and the response that motion requires priority and is incompatible with an eternal world.

Core Idea
Motion requires being preceded, so an eternal world cannot be in motion.

This lesson addresses a key objection to the argument that the world must have a beginning. If the world is eternal, why could it not have been in motion from eternity? The response invokes the nature of motion: each motion is preceded by another motion, leading to an infinite regress that is impossible. The dialogue clarifies the concept of priority in motion.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objector grants the dichotomy between motion and rest but asks why the world could not have been moving eternally.
2
Understand the response
The response states that motion requires being preceded by another and priority (al-uwaliyya) is incompatible with motion.
The term 'priority' here means that each motion must have a prior motion.
3
Analyze the counter-response
The author questions why priority would preclude a specific motion, implying that the objector's reasoning may be flawed.
The phrase 'محال مكان' is unclear but likely a marginal note.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا الحصر، لكن لم لا يجوز أن يقال إنها كانت متحركة . قوله : الحركة تقتضى المسبوقية بالغير والأولية وتنافيها . محال مكان قلنا الأولية تنافى وجود حركة معينة لسكن لم قلت إنها تنافى فى وجود حركة قبل حركة الثاني في أول أما الوجه الثاني وهو أن المجموع فعل فاعل مختار وله أول قلت لا تسلم إنه فعل فاعل مختار . بيانه أن الموجب قد يتخلف عنه الأثر إما لفوات شرط أو الحصول مانع فلم لا يجوز أن يقال المؤثر في وجود هذه الحوادث موجب بالذات إلا أن كل حادث متقدم فتقدمه شرط لأن يصدر من العلة الموجبة حادث آخر بعده بواسطة . سلمنا أنه فعل المختار لكن لا تسلم أن فعل المختار محدث ، وذلك لأن وجود الحادث وصحة تأثير المؤثر فيه عملك أبدا وإلا فقد كان متن ما لذاته ثم انقلاب ممسكا وذلك عمال وإذا كان كل واحد منهما مكنا أولا كان تأثير الصادر في وجود الأثر جائز أزلا، سلمنا أن الاجسام ما كانت متحركة فلم لا يجوز كونها ساكنة .
We grant the dichotomy, but why is it not permissible to say that they were moving? He said: motion requires being preceded by another and priority, and they are incompatible. Impossible [place]. We said: priority precludes the existence of a specific motion — why do you say it precludes the existence of a motion before a motion? As for the second aspect, that the aggregate is an act of a free agent and has a first, we say: we do not grant that it is an act of a free agent. Explanation: the necessary cause may be followed by the effect either due to a missing condition or an obstacle, so why is it not permissible to say that the effecter in the existence of these events is necessary by essence, except that every preceding event's priority is a condition for the necessary cause to produce another event after it by mediation? We grant that it is an act of the free agent, but we do not grant that the act of the free agent is temporal. That is because the existence of the event and the validity of the effecter's influence in it are always [possible?] otherwise it would be [unclear]. And if each of them is possible initially, then the effecter's influence in the existence of the effect is possible eternally. We grant that the bodies were not moving, so why is it not permissible for them to be at rest?
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections and responses regarding the possibility of an eternal world being in motion or at rest. The first objection (سلمنا الحصر) grants the dichotomy of motion/rest but asks why the world could not have been moving. The response (قوله: الحركة تقتضى المسبوقية...) is that motion requires being preceded by something else (meaning each motion must have a prior motion) and that priority (al-uwaliyya) is incompatible with motion. The author then questions this: why would priority preclude a specific motion? He shifts to the second aspect: that the entire world is an act of a free agent (fa'il mukhtar) and must have a beginning. The objector denies that it is an act of a free agent, arguing instead that the cause could be a necessary cause (muijib) that produces effects conditionally — each preceding event is a condition for the next, avoiding an infinite regress. The author then accepts that it is a free action but denies that freedom necessitates temporality; the free agent's ability to act could be eternal. Finally, the objector grants that bodies were not moving but asks why they could not have been at rest (sakina). This sets up further discussion on the next page.

Key Terms
الحركة
المسبوقية
الأولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An eternal world could be in motion without a beginning.
Correction: Motion requires a prior motion, so an eternal motion would entail an infinite regress of motions, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason given against the world being eternally in motion?
A) Motion requires a prior motion, leading to infinite regress.
B) Motion is an accident and cannot be eternal.
C) The world must have a beginning because it is composed.
D) The objector grants the dichotomy.

Key Takeaway
Motion cannot be eternal because each motion requires a prior motion, leading to an impossible infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You assume that some habits or patterns in your life are 'just the way you are' with no beginning. But this lesson shows that every motion requires a prior cause. What is the specific 'prior motion' that started one of your recurring sinful patterns? Will you trace it back today and confront it head-on?
?
The objector thought the world could be in eternal motion without a starting point. Are there areas in your spiritual life where you are waiting for change without taking the 'first motion'? What is one specific action you can take today to break that stasis, knowing that change must begin somewhere?
?
The opponent's statement that motion requires priority implies that nothing moves without a cause. Is your faith in Allah a first cause that moves your actions, or are you content with being moved by worldly influences? Commit to examining one decision today and ensuring its first cause is your love for Allah.

Aggregate as Free Act vs. Necessary Cause with Conditions
Objection and response about the cause of the world's events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the aggregate of events is an act of a free agent and the response that it could be due to a necessary cause operating with conditions.

Core Idea
A necessary cause can produce effects non-immediately if conditions are fulfilled.

This lesson examines whether the world's events are necessarily caused by a free agent or by a necessary cause that operates through conditions. The objector denies that the aggregate is an act of a free agent, arguing instead for a necessary cause where each event's priority is a condition for the next. This opens the possibility of an eternal world with temporal events.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The second aspect: the aggregate is claimed to be an act of a free agent with a first event. The objector denies this.
2
Understand the alternative explanation
The objector proposes that a necessary cause may produce effects conditionally: each preceding event is a condition for the next.
This avoids the need for a first event.
3
Reflect on the implication
If conditions can delay the effect, then an eternal cause could produce temporal events without a beginning.
This is a crucial argument for those who accept the eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Temporality of Free Action and Eternal Rest
Objections and responses on temporality and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments that free action does not necessarily imply temporality and that the world could have been at rest eternally.

Core Idea
A free agent's act could be eternal, and eternal rest is possible.

This lesson continues the debate by granting that the world is an act of a free agent but denying that this implies a temporal beginning. The objector argues that the free agent's power could be eternal, so the effect could be eternal. Finally, he asks why the world could not have been eternally at rest, challenging the assumption that rest requires a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection on temporality
Granting free agency, the objector denies that it must be temporal.
2
Understand the argument for eternal possibility
The objector says that if both the event and the agent's power are possible eternally, then the effect could be eternal.
The Arabic is unclear but the gist is that eternal possibility allows eternal actuality.
3
Examine the objection on rest
Finally, the objector asks why bodies could not have been eternally at rest, implying that rest is not impossible.

Source Text

Non-Existence as a Condition: Answering the Objection on Beginningless Events
Rebuttal of an objection about non-existence as a condition for existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how non-existence can be a condition for existence without being a cause, resolving an objection about events without a beginning.

Core Idea
Non-existence can be a condition, not a cause, for existence.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: if the non-existence of a prior event cannot cause a later event, how can we explain the dependency? The answer lies in distinguishing between a cause (ʿillah) and a condition (sharṭ). The author uses everyday examples to show that non-existence often serves as a condition, not a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and rebuttal
Read the passage carefully. First, understand the objection that non-existence cannot be a cause. Then, follow the rebuttal that distinguishes condition from cause.
Notice the key Arabic terms: تعليل (causation) and اشتراط (conditioning).
2
Identify the key distinction
The passage argues that while non-existence cannot cause existence, it can be a condition. Understand why this distinction matters in theological reasoning.
3
Examine the examples
Study the two examples: absence of clouds for sunlight, and absence of oiliness for dyeing. How do these illustrate the role of non-existence as a condition?
Reflect on how these examples relate to the original objection about beginningless events.
4
Connect to broader theology
Consider how this distinction preserves the principle that only existence can cause existence, while allowing non-existence to be a necessary condition.
This connects to discussions about divine attributes and the temporality of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حوادث لا أول لها فقد تقدم إبطاله لكنه قال قبل ذلك في المسألة التي ذكر فيها أن مدير العالم واجب الوجود هكذا حال حدوثه ذلك السابق لم يكن النديم مؤثرا بالفعل فى الحادث اللاحق وعند فنائه يصير مؤثرا فيه بالفعل فتلك المؤثرية حكم حادث ولا بد له من مؤثر ، إن كان هو الحادث الذي عدم الآن لزم تحليل الوجود بالعدم وهو محال ، فيقال له لم لا يجوز أن يكون عدم السابق بعد وجوده شرطا لوجود اللاحق ، ولا يلزم من امتناع تعليل الوجود بالعدم امتناع اشتراط الوجود بالعدم، فإن عدم الغيم شرط فى إضاءة وجه الأرض من الشمس وعدم الدسومة شرط في الصباغ الثوب من الصبغ .
As for events with no beginning (ḥawādith lā awwal lahā), its refutation has already preceded. But he said before that in the issue in which he mentioned that the manager of the world is necessarily existent, thus the case of its origination: that prior was not effectively influencing the subsequent event, and upon its perishing it becomes effectively influencing it. So that influencing is an originated ruling and must have an influencer. If it is the event that has now ceased, then analysis of existence by non-existence would follow, which is impossible. So it is said to him: Why is it not permissible that the non-existence of the prior after its existence be a condition (sharṭ) for the existence of the subsequent? And from the impossibility of causing existence by non-existence, it does not follow that it is impossible to condition existence by non-existence. For the absence of clouds is a condition for the illumination of the earth's surface by the sun, and the absence of oiliness is a condition for the dyeing of the cloth by the dye.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection related to events without a beginning. The author previously argued that the non-existence of a prior event cannot cause a subsequent one. The rebuttal points out that non-existence can serve as a condition (sharṭ) without being a cause (ʿillah). The example given is that the absence of clouds is a condition for the sun's light to brighten the earth, and the absence of oiliness is a condition for dye to color cloth. Thus, it is possible for the non-existence of a prior event to be a condition for the existence of a later one, without implying causation from non-existence.

Key Terms
حوادث لا أول لها
شرط
تعليل
اشتراط
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That non-existence cannot be involved in any way in the existence of something else.
Correction: Non-existence can be a condition for existence without being a cause, as shown by natural examples.

Checkpoint
What example is given to illustrate that non-existence can be a condition for existence?
A) The absence of clouds for sunshine
B) The absence of motion for rest
C) The absence of a cause for an effect
D) The absence of knowledge for ignorance

Key Takeaway
Non-existence can serve as a condition (sharṭ) for existence without being a cause (ʿillah), as seen in natural examples like the absence of clouds enabling sunlight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area in your life where you are fixated on what is missing—financial, relational, or spiritual—and have assumed that this lack can only mean harm? Could it be that Allah has made that very absence a necessary condition for a greater good you have not yet seen? Will you trust His wisdom over your own judgment?
?
The objection in the lesson assumed that non-existence cannot have any positive role. Reflect: In your reaction to trials, have you ever said, 'Nothing good can come from this loss'? How does this lesson’s distinction between cause and condition challenge that assumption? What would it mean to realign your heart with that truth?
?
Consider a trial you are currently facing or have faced where you experienced a significant 'absence' (e.g., of health, wealth, a loved one, or an opportunity). Can you now see that absence as a condition that might be enabling something else? What would change in your attitude toward Allah if you truly accepted that the absence is a purposeful arrangement? How can you begin to practice that acceptance today?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
6 min
The Need for a Free Agent: Individual vs Aggregate
Razi critiques the claim that every motion needs a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi argues that the need for a free agent is not self-evident for collective motion, even if it holds for individual motions.

Core Idea
Collective motion may not need a free agent.

In this lesson, we examine Imam Razi's critique of the claim that every motion requires a free agent. He points out that even if individual motions do, the collective or species of motion may not, and the argument presented lacks proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse. Then read the translation. Notice the objector's claim and Razi's response.
The passage is from Imam Razi's refutation.
2
Identify the objector's claim
Find where the objector says every motion needs a free agent. Razi says it's not self-evident and lacks evidence.
3
Understand Razi's distinction
Razi grants that individual motions might need a free agent, but for the aggregate or species, the argument by delay does not apply because they have not been proven to be delayed.
This is the key logical move.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this distinction matters for the debate on eternal motion: even if each particular motion needs a cause, the whole series might be explicable without a free agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله في الوجه الثاني من بيان امتناع كون الحركة أزلية أن كل الحركات محتاج إلى موجد مختار فغير بين بنفسه ولم يورد عليه دليلا. وقد يلوح من كلامه عند الاعتراض عليه أنه إنما قيد [؟] موجد مختار لتخلف الحركة عنه وامتناع تخلف المعلول عن العلة الموجبة، ولكنه لو سلم له هذا المسلك في كل واحد من الحركات، أما المجموع والنوع فلم يثبت كونهما متخلفين عن مؤثرهما حتى يسوغ له الدلالة بالتخلف على كون الموجد مختارا. وقد أحال في الجواب بيان امتناع كون الموجد واجبا وكون كل سابق شرطا لحصول اللاحق إلى باب إثبات القادر، وفي ذلك لم يزد على قوله: وأما حوادث لا أول لها فقد تقدم إبطاله. لكنه قال قبل ذلك في المسألة التي ذكر فيها أن مدير العالم واجب الوجود هكذا حال حدوثه: ذلك السابق لم يكن المؤثر بالفعل في الحادث اللاحق، وعند فنائه يصير مؤثرا فيه بالفعل، فتلك المؤثرية حكم حادث ولا بد له من مؤثر، إن كان هو الحادث الذي عدم الآن لزم تحليل الوجود بالعدم وهو محال. فيقال له: لم لا يجوز أن يكون عدم السابق بعد وجوده شرطا لوجود اللاحق، ولا يلزم من امتناع تعليل الوجود بالعدم امتناع اشتراط الوجود بالعدم، فإن عدم الغيم شرط في إضاءة وجه الأرض من الشمس وعدم الدسومة شرط في صباغ الثوب من الصبغ.
As for his statement in the second aspect of explaining the impossibility of motion being eternal, that every motion is in need of a free agent, it is not self-evident and he did not bring evidence for it. And it appears from his speech when objecting to it that he only restricted [to] a free agent because of the delay of motion from it and the impossibility of the effect's delay from the necessitating cause. But if this path is granted to him for each individual motion, as for the aggregate and the species, they have not been proven to be delayed from their effect so that he may use the delay to indicate that the agent is free. And he referred in the answer the explanation of the impossibility of the agent being necessary and of every prior being a condition for the occurrence of the posterior to the chapter on establishing the powerful. And in that he added nothing to his statement: 'As for events without beginning, its refutation has preceded.' But he said before that in the issue in which he mentioned that the director of the world is necessary of existence, such is the state of its occurrence: that prior was not an agent in act for the later event, and at its annihilation it becomes an agent in act, so that agency is an accidental judgment and must have an agent; if it is the event that now ceased, it would necessitate the analysis of existence by non-existence, which is impossible. So it is said to him: Why is it not permissible that the non-existence of the prior after its existence be a condition for the existence of the posterior? And from the impossibility of explaining existence by non-existence does not follow the impossibility of conditioning existence by non-existence, for the non-existence of clouds is a condition for the illumination of the earth's face from the sun, and the non-existence of greasiness is a condition for the dyeing of the garment by the dye.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage refutes an objection regarding the second aspect of the impossibility of motion being eternal. The objector claimed that every motion requires a free agent, but Imam Razi argues this is not self-evident and lacks proof. Even if one accepts the need for a free agent for each individual motion, the aggregate or species of motion may not depend on such an agent in the same way. The objector then attempts to show that the agent must be free by appealing to the delay of the effect, but Razi points out that the collective motion has not been proven to be delayed. The objector refers to another discussion about the impossibility of the agent being necessary, but Razi counters with an example: the non-existence of a prior condition can be a condition for the existence of a subsequent event, without implying that non-existence causes existence. He illustrates with mundane examples: the absence of clouds is a condition for sunlight reaching the earth, and the absence of grease is a condition for dye penetrating cloth.

Key Terms
حركة
أزلية
موجد مختار
علة موجبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi, why does the objector's argument about every motion needing a free agent fail for the collective motion?
A) Because collective motions are not delayed from their effect.
B) Because collective motions do not exist.
C) Because the free agent argument only applies to eternal motions.
D) Because the objector did not define motion.

Key Takeaway
Even if individual motions require a free agent, the aggregate or species may not, so the argument for a free agent is incomplete.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector assumed a free agent is needed because of delay, but Razi showed that this doesn't necessarily apply to the whole. Where in your life have you taken a limited truth about God and applied it too broadly? What would it mean to let God be free beyond your categories?
?
Razi points out that the objector's claim is not self-evident. Are there beliefs you hold about God that you've accepted without evidence, simply because they feel intuitive? Will you examine them today?
?
The argument for a free agent from delay works for individual events, but not for the whole system. Do you trust God's wisdom even when the big picture seems to lack the same structure you see in small events? Ask yourself: Am I willing to surrender my need for neat causality in the face of God's absolute sovereignty?
